<div id="review_278875001" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/278875001" />
    <a title="Tony" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33771-tony"><img alt="Tony" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1292869016p2/33771.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/278875001?book_show_action=true">Feb 16, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tony" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tony" href="/user/show/33771-tony">Tony</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781590173022" href="/book/show/6145869-season-of-migration-to-the-north">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Tony by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/3926982-shovelmonkey1">Shovelmonkey1</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33771-tony?shelf=nyrb-classics">nyrb-classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33771-tony?shelf=sudanese">sudanese</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer278875001" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10942591845900100082">The Sudanese narrator of this novel returns after seven years study in England. He is received warmly. Bint Majzoub put it to him in her peculiar earthy fashion, “We were afraid you’d bring back with you an uncircumcised infidel for a wife.”<br/><br/>He brings instead a developing awareness for Sudan in its post-colonial state, and for men like himself and the enigmatic Moustafa Sa’eed who have tasted both lives. He sees a Sudan where the British left because they had no need to stay:<br/><br/><i>Be sure, though, that</i></span>
  <span id="freeText10942591845900100082" style="display:none">The Sudanese narrator of this novel returns after seven years study in England. He is received warmly. Bint Majzoub put it to him in her peculiar earthy fashion, “We were afraid you’d bring back with you an uncircumcised infidel for a wife.”<br /><br />He brings instead a developing awareness for Sudan in its post-colonial state, and for men like himself and the enigmatic Moustafa Sa’eed who have tasted both lives. He sees a Sudan where the British left because they had no need to stay:<br /><br /><i>Be sure, though, that they will direct our affairs from afar. This is because they have left behind people who think as they do.</i><br /><br />And…<br /><br /><i>The schools were started so as to teach us to say ‘Yes’ in their language.</i><br /><br />What was also imported was “the germ of the greatest European violence, as seen on the Somme and at Verdun, the like of which the world has never previously known, the germ of a deadly disease that struck them more than a thousand years ago.”<br /><br />Yes, history repeats. The Romans invaded Britain. Britain would, in turn, move south. Rinse, repeat. Presidents should read this.<br /><br />A love story follows the narrator’s observations. Actually, love <i>stories</i>. They are not sentimental and probably allegorical.<br /><br /><i>Love? Love does not do this. This is hatred.</i><br /><br />So this book is both a warning and a blues.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10942591845900100082" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1029891748" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1029891748" />
    <a title="Zanna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22591633-zanna"><img alt="Zanna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1512813535p2/22591633.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1029891748?book_show_action=true">Aug 18, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Zanna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Zanna" href="/user/show/22591633-zanna">Zanna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781590173022" href="/book/show/6145869-season-of-migration-to-the-north">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22591633-zanna?shelf=translated">translated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22591633-zanna?shelf=g-african-reads">g-african-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1029891748" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17451399000187927323">Laila Lalami reveals in the introduction to this slender volume that Tayeb Salih wrote in Arabic as a matter of principle, as <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/51936.Ng_g__wa_Thiong_o' title='Ngũgĩ wa Thiong’o' rel='nofollow noopener'>Ngũgĩ wa Thiong’o</a> decided to write in Gikuyu, explaining his decision in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/152622.Decolonising_the_Mind_The_Politics_of_Language_in_African_Literature' title='Decolonising the Mind The Politics of Language in African Literature by Ngũgĩ wa Thiong’o' rel='nofollow noopener'>Decolonising the Mind: The Politics of Language in African Literature</a> with force and clarity: English is the language of colonisation, it cuts off the native from her culture, her people, enforces a way of knowing, a mode of relation and experience. Understanding this, I treasure the art of translati</span>
  <span id="freeText17451399000187927323" style="display:none">Laila Lalami reveals in the introduction to this slender volume that Tayeb Salih wrote in Arabic as a matter of principle, as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/51936.Ng_g__wa_Thiong_o" title="Ngũgĩ wa Thiong’o" rel="nofollow noopener">Ngũgĩ wa Thiong’o</a> decided to write in Gikuyu, explaining his decision in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/152622.Decolonising_the_Mind_The_Politics_of_Language_in_African_Literature" title="Decolonising the Mind The Politics of Language in African Literature by Ngũgĩ wa Thiong’o" rel="nofollow noopener">Decolonising the Mind: The Politics of Language in African Literature</a> with force and clarity: English is the language of colonisation, it cuts off the native from her culture, her people, enforces a way of knowing, a mode of relation and experience. Understanding this, I treasure the art of translation that allows me, a monolingual reader, to commune with minds and worlds across the rivers of word. Lalami goes on to lament that there is very little Arabic literature well-translated into English. Last year I was privileged to read two books by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1424397.Radwa_Ashour" title="Radwa Ashour" rel="nofollow noopener">Radwa Ashour</a>, so I know my problems with this book had nothing to do with its original language or translation.<br /><br />The theme of language did appeal to me. When the protagonist notes that the object of his fascination, Mustafa Sa'eed, had 'not a single Arabic book' in his collection, I understand that the disease of colonisation (which is identified as entering British people at the time of the Roman invasion) is carried by the word. I couldn't help noticing that the woman sitting next to me on the tube while I was reading this was reading a books called 'Life in the UK: a Guide for New Residents, intended as a study guide for the 'Life in the UK' residency/citizenship test, and the chapter she was on was called 'A Long and Illustrious History', a title which needs no comment. Still, I found faith that no immigrant could actually believe that fat, glossy, expensive book of propaganda. That the colonised subject, the racialised-as-other person, is awake and thinking, is well able to turn the coloniser's ignorance against her, is an idea taken up by Salih. Mustafa uses the language of Orientalist exotification to stoke the fantasies of women, so that he can have sexual affairs with them.<br /><br />My problems started more or less here. I appreciate that the people Mustafa is able to 'prey' on are those whose power is circumscribed (women). However, the text increasingly becomes an unpleasant male fantasy unable to give the women voice and intent. Mustafa collaborates with the women in exotifying him, but they are seen as victims. This could be read as a matter of viewpoint, but the narrative concurs with Mustafa's violent, dominating scheme, by killing the women off. His most intense relationship seems to be an entanglement of two psychopathic* personalities, which traumatises him and is fatal to her.<br /><br />(this paragraph is a bit spoilery but I recommend reading it anyway haha)<br />In the protagonist's native village Mustafa's influence causes havoc in his absence, as his former wife, Hosna, forced into a second marriage, defends herself against marital rape. I must own my standpoint here as a White British feminist, but I believe I speak with Black and Brown feminists &amp; allies of all faiths &amp; none when I assert that the bottom line is that no means no. So what am I to make of the brutal punishment the narrative metes out to Hosna? At least one woman stands with her in solidarity, but this character takes no previous or subsequent role in the text. The only other interesting female character, Bint Majzoub, who behaves 'like a man' takes a leading role in enforcing the patriarchal order. It is as if the disease of colonialism rather than native spirit, conviction or self-esteem caused Hosna to stand up for herself and destroyed her and others. There is no hope. The protagonist's final decision seems meaningless after this climax.<br /><br />I like unconventional narrative structures, and Salih's use of the story-within-a-story agreeably recalled, say, the Thousand and One Nights (which is repeatedly referenced). When Mustafa narrates, he constantly uses an 'exotic' image of himself as a desert nomad on the move 'saddling my camel' and so on, helping to orient(!) me in the slippery story as the protagonist slides into Mustafa's and his own memories frequently. This time-play was enjoyable, but as well as bridling at all the denouments, I was irritated by Mustafa's style, and the repetitious imagery wasn't to my taste. The book it most recalled to me was <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/45967.The_Blind_Owl" title="The Blind Owl by Sadegh Hedayat" rel="nofollow noopener">The Blind Owl</a>, another classic which I strongly disliked for its poetic style.<br /><br />My favourite scene, the spontaneous party in the desert, combined a euphoric sense of instant community, the kind of thing that (almost) <a href="https://roseannastar.wordpress.com/2013/09/01/one-person-is-a-fiction-2/" rel="nofollow noopener">happens when you go to the theatre</a>, with a dreamlike ghostliness – the atmosphere that makes the classic cartoon <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=HHCZhUZXhZU" rel="nofollow noopener">'The Snowman'</a> so haunting. The most enjoyable thing about this novel for me was the evocation of life along the Nile, the river and the desert and the lively life of the village, which the protagonist recognises and reveres in the person of his friend Mahjoub. In so far as Mahjoub embodies this vision, it is also attacked by the colonial disease that infects the protagonist; what I learn to love is threatened, but I will not accept that all is lost.<br /><br />Antidote: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10335337.Mr__Fox" title="Mr. Fox by Helen Oyeyemi" rel="nofollow noopener">Mr. Fox</a> by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/80808.Helen_Oyeyemi" title="Helen Oyeyemi" rel="nofollow noopener">Helen Oyeyemi</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="17451399000187927323" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_240151923" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/240151923" />
    <a title="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2190636-joselito-honestly-and-brilliantly"><img alt="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349701607p2/2190636.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/240151923?book_show_action=true">Nov 27, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" href="/user/show/2190636-joselito-honestly-and-brilliantly">Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/122659.Season_of_Migration_to_the_North">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer240151923" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13617123901113833134">Buy this book. Buy it new, don't get a second hand copy from a thrift shop. Buy the Penguin Classics edition.<br/><br/>This was first published in Arabic in September 1966. It was banned in Egypt for almost thirty years until an Egyptian publisher re-published a low-priced edition of about a hundred thousand copies which sold out in a matter of days.<br/><br/>A publishing house in Beirut had been publishing this since 1970. Hundreds of thousands of copies had been distributed throughout the Arab world. In some of t</span>
  <span id="freeText13617123901113833134" style="display:none">Buy this book. Buy it new, don't get a second hand copy from a thrift shop. Buy the Penguin Classics edition.<br /><br />This was first published in Arabic in September 1966. It was banned in Egypt for almost thirty years until an Egyptian publisher re-published a low-priced edition of about a hundred thousand copies which sold out in a matter of days.<br /><br />A publishing house in Beirut had been publishing this since 1970. Hundreds of thousands of copies had been distributed throughout the Arab world. In some of these countries it is banned, then unbanned. It is permanently banned, however, in all the Gulf states.<br /><br />It had been translated into Russian and sold millions. Translated into Hebrew without the author's permission, it was a bestseller. The French translation made Francois Mauriac say: "We have never read anything like this before, we who have read everything."<br /><br />All throughout these, the ringing cash registers and the accolades, the author has earned almost nothing. He laments: "It is true that I have become well-known all over the place but of course, renown without recompense is no consolation for a writer."<br /><br />But there is hope now that Penguin had published it, and with his literary agent, the author is confident that maybe he shall now "start to derive some material benefits from this onerous and not entirely felicitious pursuit of novel writing." (I suppose he meant "felicitous" but "felicitious" is what appears in his introduction to the Penguin edition).<br /><br />That is why this is the book you should not beg for, borrow or steal. Buy it. don't let a great writer die destitute. Show your solidarity with brave people like him who write stuffs like this.<br /><br />Then you'll have the pleasure of meeting Mustafa Sa'eed. Handsome, intelligent and black, once the pride of Khartoum, he had gone to London after the First World War and regaled the British with his wit and exotic looks. Implied was his proud African dick which is the envy of Asians and Caucasians alike. He hunted white, female Londoners ripe for seduction like a bushman tracking down deers. He knew these women have pre-conceived notions about black men like him which stimulate their appetite for romance and adventure, so he played along with them, lying about everything including his name. He narrates his conquests with powerful sexual metaphors. Morsels of delight of which I shall now give you a sample (to make you buy the book):<br /><br />"Yes. It was summer--they said that they had not known a summer like it for a hundred years. I left my house on a Saturday, sniffing the air, feeling I was about to start upon a great hunt. I reached Speakers' Corner in Hyde Park. It was packed with people. I stood listening from afar to a speaker from the West Indies talking about the colour problem. Suddenly my eyes came to rest on a woman who was craning her neck to catch a glimpse of the speaker so that her dress was lifted above her knees exposing two shapely, bronzed legs. Yes, this was my prey. I walked up to her, like a boat heading towards the rapids. I stood beside her and pressed up close against her till I felt her warmth pervading me. I breathed in the odour of her body....I was so close to her that, becoming aware of me, she turned to me suddenly. I smiled into her face--a smile the outcome of which I knew not, except that I was determined that it should not go to waste. I also laughed lest the surprise in her face should turn to animosity. Then she smiled. I stood beside her for about a quarter of an hour, laughing when the speaker's words made her laugh--loudly so that she might be affected by the contagion of it. Then came the moment when I felt that she and I had become like a mare and foal running in harmony side by side. A sound, as though it were not my voice, issued from my throat: 'What about a drink, away from this crowd and heat?' She turned her head in astonishment. This time I smiled--a broad innocent smile so that I might change astonishment into, at least, curiosity. Meanwhile I closely examined her face: each one of her features increased my conviction that this was my prey. With the instinct of a gambler I knew that this was a decisive moment. At this moment everything was possible. My smile changed to a gladness I could scarcely keep in rein as she said: 'Yes, why not?' We walked along together; she beside me, a glittering figure of bronze under the July sun, a city of secrets and rapture. I was pleased she laughed so freely. Such a woman--there are many of her type in Europe--knows no fear; they accept life with gaiety and curiosity. And I am a thirsty desert, a wilderness of southern desires. As we drank tea, she asked me about my home. I related to her fabricated stories about deserts of golden sands and jungles where non-existent animals called out to one another. I told her that the streets of my country teemed with elephants and lions and that during siesta time crocodiles crawled through it. Half-credulous, half-disbelieving, she listened to me, laughing and closing her eyes, her cheeks reddening. Sometimes she would hear me out in silence, a Christian sympathy in her eyes. There came a moment when I felt I had been transformed in her eyes into a naked, primitive creature, a spear in one hand and arrows in the other, hunting elephants and lions in the jungles. This was fine. Curiosity had changed into gaiety, and gaiety to sympathy, and when I stir the still pool in its depths the sympathy will be transformed into a desire upon whose taut strings I shall play as I wish. 'What race are you?' she asked me. 'Are you African or Asian?'<br /><br />"'I'm like Othello--Arab-African,' I said to her.<br /><br />"'Yes,' she said, looking into my face. 'Your nose is like the noses of Arabs in pictures, but your hair isn't soft and jet black like that of Arabs.'<br /><br />"'Yes, that's me. My face is Arab like the desert of the Empty Quarter, while my head is African and teems with a mischievous childishness.'<br /><br />"'You put things in such a funny way,' she said laughing.<br /><br />"The conversation led us to my family, and I told her--without lying this time--that I had grown up without a father. Then, returning to my lies, I gave her such a terrifying descriptions of how I had lost my parents that I saw the tears well up in her eyes. I told her I was six years old at the time when my parents were drowned with thirty other people in a boat taking them from one bank of the Nile to the other. Here something occurred which was better than expressions of pity; pity in such instances is an emotion with uncertain consequences. Her eyes brightened and she cried out ecstatically:<br /><br />"'The Nile.'<br /><br />"'Yes, the Nile.'<br /><br />"'Then you live on the banks of the Nile?'<br /><br />"'Yes. Our house is right on the bank of the Nile, so that when I'm lying on my bed at night I put my hand out of the window and idly play with the Nile waters till sleep overtakes me.'<br /><br />Mr Mustafa, the bird has fallen into the snare. The Nile, that snake god, has gained a new victim. The city has changed into a woman. It would be but a day or a week before I would pitch tent, driving my tent peg into the mountain summit. You, my lady, may not know, but you--like Carnarvon when he entered Tutankhamen's tomb--have been infected with a deadly disease which has come from you know not where and which will bring about your destruction, be it sooner or later. My store of hackneyed phrases is inexhaustible. I felt the flow of conversation firmly in my hands, like the reins of an obedient mare: I pull at them and she stops, I shake them and she advances; I move them and she moves subject to my will, to left or to right.<br /><br />"'Two hours have passed without my being aware of them,' I said to her. 'I've not felt such happiness for a long time. And there's so much left for me to say to you and you to me. What would you say to having dinner together and continuing the conversation?'<br /><br />For a while she remained silent. I was not alarmed for I felt that satanic warmth under my diaphragm, and when I feel it I know that I am in full command of the situation. No, she would not say no. 'This is an extraordinary meeting,' she said. 'A man I don't know invites me out. It's not right, but--' She was silent. 'Yes, why not?' she then said. 'There's nothing to tell from your face you're a cannibal.'<br /><br />"'You'll find I'm an aged crocodile who's lost its teeth,' I said to her, a wave of joy stirring in the roots of my heart. 'I wouldn't have the strength to eat you even if I wanted to.' I reckoned I was at least fifteen years her junior, for she was a woman in the region of forty, whose body--whatever the experiences she had undergone--time had treated kindly. The fine wrinkles on her forehead and at the corners of her mouth told one not that she had grown old, but that she had ripened.<br /><br />Only then did I ask her name.<br /><br />"'Isabella Seymour,' she said.<br /><br />"I repeated it twice, rolling it round my tongue as though eating a pear.<br /><br />"'And what's your name?'<br /><br />"'I'm--Amin. Amin Hassan.'<br /><br />"'I shall call you Hassan.'<br /><br />"With the grills and wine her features relaxed and there gushed forth--upon me--a love she felt for the whole world. I wasn't so much concerned with her love for the world, or for the cloud of sadness that crossed her face from time to time, as I was with the redness of her tongue when she laughed, the fulness of her lips and the secrets lurking in the abyss of her mouth. I pictured her obscenely naked as she said: 'Life is full of pain, yet we must be optimistic and face life with courage.'<br /><br />"Yes, I now know that in the rough wisdom that issues from the mouths of simple people lies our whole hope of salvation. A tree grows simply and your grandfather has lived and will die simply. That is the secret. You are right, my lady: courage and optimism. But until the meek inherit the earth, until the armies are disbanded, the lamb grazes in peace beside the wolf and the child plays water-polo in the river with the crocodile, until that time of happiness and love comes along, I for one shall continue to express myself in this twisted manner. And when, puffing, I reach the mountain peak and implant the banner, collect my breath and rest--that, my lady, is an ecstasy greater to me than love, than happiness. Thus I mean you no harm, except to the extent that the sea is harmful when ships are wrecked against its rocks, and to the extent that the lightning is harmful when it rends a tree in two. This last idea converged in my mind on the tiny hairs on her right arm near to the wrist, and I noticed that the hair on her arms was thicker than with most women, and this led my thoughts to other hair. It would certainly be as soft and abundant as cypress-grass on the banks of a stream...."<br /><br />If anyone of you thinks this is a sex book, and you like sex books, then by all means think this is a sex book and buy several copies for yourself and your friends. But to those of you who go for more meaningful literary pursuits, rest assured that there is more to this novel than Mustafa Sa'eed's erotic adventures. This is a post-colonial novel with political undertones and there is also a murder mystery here. Ideas and cultures likewise clash like massive planets exploding a kaleidoscope of colors in space, so varied that you wouldn't know where to look. The ending is also quite disturbing but don't worry, it would be the time you'd be winding up with this book, so the disturbance won't cause you any annoyance at all.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13617123901113833134" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<span id="freeTextContainer6191477000723966633">Mustafa Sa'eed and the unnamed narrator of the novel, <i>Season of Migration to the North</i>, represent the debauched and romantic Oriental view of the East. The Occidents carry their unilateral image everywhere they go. For the Orients and sometimes, even for the Occidents since they have internalised the views bombarded upon them, age, gender, educational background, religious and ethnic differences, among others, don't seem to matter and they are often seen in one light - the embodiment of the anti</span>
  <span id="freeText6191477000723966633" style="display:none">Mustafa Sa'eed and the unnamed narrator of the novel, <i>Season of Migration to the North</i>, represent the debauched and romantic Oriental view of the East. The Occidents carry their unilateral image everywhere they go. For the Orients and sometimes, even for the Occidents since they have internalised the views bombarded upon them, age, gender, educational background, religious and ethnic differences, among others, don't seem to matter and they are often seen in one light - the embodiment of the antithesis of the West. <br /><br />Tayeb Salih is a writer who has been able to master the art of compact and realistic storytelling. The hierarchical relationship among superior and inferior rules the text, be it for the relationship between the West and the East, the relationship between men and women or the relationship between native intellectuals or natives. This is a novel that must be read not only in view of Sudanese upheaval, but also from the perspective of the world which almost always encourages hierarchy in their daily lives. </span>

<div id="review_981353760" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/981353760" />
    <a title="Nabse Bamato" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30631601-nabse-bamato"><img alt="Nabse Bamato" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442973857p2/30631601.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/981353760?book_show_action=true">Jun 29, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nabse Bamato" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nabse Bamato" href="/user/show/30631601-nabse-bamato">Nabse Bamato</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30631601-nabse-bamato?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30631601-nabse-bamato?shelf=africa">africa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30631601-nabse-bamato?shelf=east-africa">east-africa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30631601-nabse-bamato?shelf=diaspora-challenge">diaspora-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30631601-nabse-bamato?shelf=round-the-world-52-only-one-from-e">round-the-world-52-only-one-from-e</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer981353760" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18144734609255593992">It is easy to see why Season of Migration to the North has become recognised as a classic. Much better minds than mine, who are eminently qualified to comment on the novel have dissected it and analysed it and I would not wish to try and add to the discussion – it is more than is possible in a short review. However, I can tell you what I liked about it, and, as the 5 star rating shows, I did like it a lot. <br/><br/>The basic story is simple; a young (north) Sudanese man, who spent 7 years in Europe - and</span>
  <span id="freeText18144734609255593992" style="display:none">It is easy to see why Season of Migration to the North has become recognised as a classic. Much better minds than mine, who are eminently qualified to comment on the novel have dissected it and analysed it and I would not wish to try and add to the discussion – it is more than is possible in a short review. However, I can tell you what I liked about it, and, as the 5 star rating shows, I did like it a lot. <br /><br />The basic story is simple; a young (north) Sudanese man, who spent 7 years in Europe - and subsequently ends up working in Khartoum - returns to his village to find a new resident, Mustafa Sae’ed, living there. He is fascinated by this man, whose life story bears no small resemblance to his own, and attempts to discover more about Mustafa’s past. Their lives become strongly linked and by the end of the novel, circumstances bind them inextricably together. The writing style is, loosely, stream of consciousness (helping to tie the two stories together) and is beautifully evocative in its descriptions and imagery. Maybe it’s my background as an African (although not Sudanese) living in Europe but so much of the description feels familiar and the places throughout the story feel completely real. I can see them as I am reading.<br /><br />It seems to me essentially to be a novel about change. It is no accident that the village is located at a point where the Nile alters its direction. And, remembering that it was written in the 1960s , it is interesting to reflect how familiar some of the issues it addresses still feel. <br /><br />The back page of the edition I read describes it as “a powerful and evocative examination of colonization in two vastly different worlds.” This is doubtless true; there are numerous passages discussing various aspects of colonisation from a variety of viewpoints. In particular, the relative values of the traditional ways of making decisions via local committees are contrasted with civil service and working within the British system adopted in Khartoum. And the impact of colonisation is discussed from many different viewpoints, by both major and minor characters.<br /><br />However, in addition to that, the novel is an exploration of relationships between men and women during the sexual revolution of the 1960s in Europe. Women are significantly downtrodden throughout the story (in fact the whole novel starts by being addressed to “gentlemen”) and, interestingly, the one strong female character in the novel is from the village at the bend of the Nile (although arguably her strength comes from her behaving like a man). The discussions are complicated by an examination of European women’s relationships with African men (women do not come out of this well!) and this is contrasted with the relationships between men and women back in the village in Sudan. As I said, there is much to discuss and it would need several readings for me even to get close to forming a coherent thesis.<br /><br />However, as well as being an intellectual discussion of key issues in the 1960s, this is a beautifully written novel (hats off too to the translator). I would like to finish with a wonderful passage from the very first 2 pages, just to give a flavour of the writing. This is after the narrator returns to the village after being away for seven years studying in Europe, and has just woken up from his first night’s sleep in the bedroom of his childhood.<br /><br />“I listened intently to the wind: that indeed was a sound well known to me, a sound which in our village possessed a merry whispering – the sound of the wind passing through palm trees is different from when it passes through fields of corn. I heard the cooing of the turtle-dove and I looked through the window at the palm tree standing in the courtyard of our house and I knew that all was still well with life. I looked at its strong straight trunk, at its roots that strike down into the ground, at the green branches hanging down loosely over its top, and I experienced a feeling of assurance. I felt not like a storm-swept feather but like that palm tree, a being with a background, with roots, with a purpose.”<br /><br />Magical.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18144734609255593992" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_217626845" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/217626845" />
    <a title="Edita" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5685453-edita"><img alt="Edita" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595755309p2/5685453.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/217626845?book_show_action=true">Oct 01, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<span id="freeTextContainer12961579380099810175">The world goes on whether we choose for it to do so or in defiance of us. And I, like millions of mankind, walk and move, generally by force of habit, in a long caravan that ascends and descends, encamps, and then proceeds on its way.   <span id="freeText12961579380099810175" style="display:none">The world goes on whether we choose for it to do so or in defiance of us. And I, like millions of mankind, walk and move, generally by force of habit, in a long caravan that ascends and descends, encamps, and then proceeds on its way. Life in this caravan is not altogether bad. You no doubt are aware of this. The going may be hard by day, the wilderness sweeping out before us like shoreless seas; we pour with sweat, our throats are patched with thirst, and we reach the frontier beyond which we<br />think we cannot go. Then the sun sets, the air grows cool, and millions of stars twinkle in the sky. We eat and drink and the singer of the caravan breaks into song. Some of us pray in a group behind the Sheikh, others form ourselves into circles to dance and sing and clap. Above us the sky is warm and compassionate. Sometimes we travel by night for as long as we have a mind to, and when the white thread is distinguished from the black we say ‘When dawn breaks the travelers are thankful that they have journeyed by night.’ If occasionally we are deceived by a mirage, and if our heads, feverish from the action of heat and thirst, sometimes bubble with ideas devoid of any basis of validity no harm is done. The specters of night dissolve with the dawn, the fever of day is cooled by the night breeze. Is there any alternative?</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer11077937014418699378">Having just read this for a 4th or more time, I still don't quite know how to talk about it. <br/><br/>What I can tell you: its very readable; its a page turner; it sits in the gap between prose and poetry; its about the relationship between desire.   <span id="freeText11077937014418699378" style="display:none">Having just read this for a 4th or more time, I still don't quite know how to talk about it. <br /><br />What I can tell you: its very readable; its a page turner; it sits in the gap between prose and poetry; its about the relationship between desire. I think this is a meditation on the nature of sexual desire in the contact zone between the first and third worlds.<br /><br />Written in Arabic in the mid 1960s and translated into English in 1969, the novel has not at all become dated -- themes of desire cross with those of gender, colonialism, neo-colonialism, and the meaning of life. <br /><br />My thin review hardly does justice to this tone poem of a wrenching novel. It stays with me for years on end. <br /><br />I might be well read with Gordimer's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/96334.The_Pickup" title="The Pickup by Nadine Gordimer" rel="nofollow noopener"> The Pickup</a>. <br /><br />Here is the translator's introduction to the 1989 edition:<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.arabworldbooks.com/Readers2004/articles/tayebsalehE.html">http://www.arabworldbooks.com/Readers...</a> <br /><br /></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3367984833" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13816799470665216018">An interesting and discordant read. The book is well-written, daring and controversial. Everything a classic should be. It explores themes of identity, colonialism and multiculturalism, and deploys them in typical symbolic mold which is apt to make the reader think and ponder about them. That being said, it leaves a bitter taste for it's over-the-top pontification of sexual exploits, to the point where it stops being part and of the story and starts to be a self-indulgent act.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText5644898300398157345" style="display:none">A novella about a Sudanese man who returns from studying abroad and meets a sex-crazed psychopath who also lived in Europe and wants to tell the narrator all about his encounters with women, aka “his prey,” in which he encouraged them to exoticize him and finally drove them all to suicide with the power of his penis. Except one, whom he married, then murdered (not a spoiler). For real.<br /><br />Okay, look, this is a well-written book that manages to say a lot about colonialism in relatively few pages (though the writing is on the dense side so it’ll take longer than you may expect). Also, most of it is spent in the head of the narrator, who seems like a thoughtful and sensible person. Unfortunately, none of the women in it bear more than a passing resemblance to real human beings. From a cultural literacy standpoint I’m glad to have read this, but it left me feeling icky.</span>

<div id="review_1659429361" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1659429361" />
    <a title="Inderjit Sanghera" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/900800-inderjit-sanghera"><img alt="Inderjit Sanghera" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1564826235p2/900800.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1659429361?book_show_action=true">Jun 05, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Inderjit Sanghera" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Inderjit Sanghera" href="/user/show/900800-inderjit-sanghera">Inderjit Sanghera</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1659429361" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer963256774612396078">‘Season of Migration to the North’ is the story of a prodigiously talented and intelligent young Sudanese man, Mustafa Sa’eed, and his life in post-war England. The novel is replete with intensely poetic passages and beautiful metaphors-think of it as being like a modern day Arabian Nights, which explores post-colonial identity and the cult of orientalism; in many ways, Mustafa, cruel, capricious, exotic and lascivious, symbolises all this fetishisation of the ‘Orient’ by the West-Mustafa perpet</span>
  <span id="freeText963256774612396078" style="display:none">‘Season of Migration to the North’ is the story of a prodigiously talented and intelligent young Sudanese man, Mustafa Sa’eed, and his life in post-war England. The novel is replete with intensely poetic passages and beautiful metaphors-think of it as being like a modern day Arabian Nights, which explores post-colonial identity and the cult of orientalism; in many ways, Mustafa, cruel, capricious, exotic and lascivious, symbolises all this fetishisation of the ‘Orient’ by the West-Mustafa perpetuates and plays upon this, but gradually loses his sense of self beneath the myriad of masks and disguises which he weaves about himself, until he ceases to become a man, but more an ideal, a symbol of Eastern decadence, of its savagery and seduction-the story is in many ways an analogy of the colonial mindset and he West’s attempt to ‘civilize’ the barbarism of the East. Mustafa is, however, not a particularly sympathetic character-not because of his seemingly endless string of seductions-although the story does seem to descend into the lowest realms of men’s fantasies at points, but more because of his callous indifference to those around him, to the women he seduces and the sense of cynicism he exudes. This is, of course, partially reflective of the fact that Mustafa is presented to us via the eyes of the narrator, whose grasp of reality can at times be tenuous and who creates an image of Mustafa in the same way that many of the English characters did. The novel is by no means perfect, some of the descriptions of Mustafa’s relationships with English women can become juvenile, and in the case of his relationship with Jean Morris, verging on the ridiculous, but is redeemed by the poetry of the prose style and power of the story. </span>
  <a data-text-id="963256774612396078" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1488607057" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1488607057" />
    <a title="Bloodorange" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23066833-bloodorange"><img alt="Bloodorange" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1386872100p2/23066833.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1488607057?book_show_action=true">Jan 01, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bloodorange" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bloodorange" href="/user/show/23066833-bloodorange">Bloodorange</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9788362122042" href="/book/show/13081145-sezon-migracji-na-p-noc">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23066833-bloodorange?shelf=africa">africa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23066833-bloodorange?shelf=library">library</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1488607057" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10281935464307957095">Oh. Lord. How does one review a book which culminates in <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[three highly symbolic and parallel deaths <a class='jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction'>(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> and <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[an attempt at suicide, which symbolizes character’s rebirth <a class='jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction'>(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>?<br/><br/>My experience with <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/669780.Season_of_Migration_to_the_North' title='Season of Migration to the North by Tayeb Salih' rel='nofollow noopener'>Season of Migration to the North</a> was somewhat similar to reading <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/232937._The_Time_of_the_Doves__By_Rodoreda__Merce__Author__Paperback_on_01_Oct_1986' title='(The Time of the Doves) By Rodoreda, Merce (Author) Paperback on 01-Oct-1986 by Mercè Rodoreda' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Time of the Doves</a>, which – just like this book – is hailed the best book of the twentieth century written in its language: I approached both with expectations which weren’t met. I feel, h</span>
  <span id="freeText10281935464307957095" style="display:none">Oh. Lord. How does one review a book which culminates in <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[three highly symbolic and parallel deaths <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> and <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[an attempt at suicide, which symbolizes character’s rebirth <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>?<br /><br />My experience with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/669780.Season_of_Migration_to_the_North" title="Season of Migration to the North by Tayeb Salih" rel="nofollow noopener">Season of Migration to the North</a> was somewhat similar to reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/232937._The_Time_of_the_Doves__By_Rodoreda__Merce__Author__Paperback_on_01_Oct_1986" title="(The Time of the Doves) By Rodoreda, Merce (Author) Paperback on 01-Oct-1986 by Mercè Rodoreda" rel="nofollow noopener">The Time of the Doves</a>, which – just like this book – is hailed the best book of the twentieth century written in its language: I approached both with expectations which weren’t met. I feel, however, that I must give <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/669780.Season_of_Migration_to_the_North" title="Season of Migration to the North by Tayeb Salih" rel="nofollow noopener">Season of Migration to the North</a> the benefit of the doubt: whenever I read a book from outside my cultural circle, and consider its narrative construction to be less than effective, I fear I’m missing some references I would have probably known if I were more familiar with the given writing tradition/ period/ culture. The two storylines the book contains - Mustafa's and our unnamed narrator's – are very different and very differently handled, structure- and style-wise, but the narrative voice is compelling.<br /><br />I think the ending (referenced under the second spoiler alert of this review) is the pointer as to what happened to the protagonist, Mustafa. After I shook the inevitable associations with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/929782.Martin_Eden" title="Martin Eden by Jack London" rel="nofollow noopener">Martin Eden</a>, I understood it was about Mustafa, not the unnamed narrator. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Mustafa either stages this suicide, or actually wants to die (hence the letter and leaving his wife and sons in the narrator’s care), but changes his mind, similarly to the narrator.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> Waiting for the Bluebeard chamber to open? Clichéd. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Also, the concept of Mustafa’s library – a treasury of useless books in the desert, counterbalanced by photos of the ‘conquered’ British women, seems to me a romanticized symbol of colonization/ counter-colonization through education, reshaping/ owning ideas received from colonizers, and sex. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. <br /><br />Regarding the books’ representation of women: I am not revolted by it, merely unsatisfied. Hosna, Mustafa’s widow <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[(despite violent protestations of Arab scholars, I believe Mustafa simply upped and left, becoming responsible for yet another woman’s death. More precisely, he shares the responsibility with the narrator, who refuses to marry Hosna, who probably sees what’s coming and knows Wad Raees wants to add her to his collection of wives. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> resists remarriage, and later consummation of marriage with Wad Raees – yet somehow this seems less of a protest of a 20th century woman to how she’s treated by an oppressive society, nor does it feel entirely realistic; it becomes a myth. Hosna’s story makes me think of Arthurian legends. I see her fate as being killed by her environment, and then symbolically killed once more.<br /><br />Another thing I don’t fully understand is the biased representation of British women. The main message, to me, is that in the 1930s, a white woman would have to be seriously unstable (Ann), dying (Isabella), or politically involved (Sheila) to have sex with a dark-skinned man, and insane (Jean) to marry one. (The book’s representation of ‘filthy liberalism’ and where it can lead is interesting – not just today, when Donald Trump becomes the 45th president of the U.S.)<br /><br />Regarding the, erm, tangle of interrelated tragic events at the end of the novel (references under spoiler one), I can see a parallel between Mustafa’s British marriage (obsession, resistance, withholding sex, the event that shall not be named) and the marriage between Wad Raees and Hosna (pursuit, resistance, withholding sex, the event that shall not be named x 2). <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Hosna’s killing of Wad Raees seems to mirror Mustafa’s killing of Jane (interestingly, in both cases we see for ourselves, or hear from another character, that the dead were ‘asking for it’, albeit in different ways). All of the three murders are strongly sexualized – meaning not only the context, but also the symbolic charge (female genital mutilation in reverse, for instance). <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>
  <a data-text-id="10281935464307957095" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1846116362" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1846116362" />
    <a title="Missy J" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7406091-missy-j"><img alt="Missy J" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1481102743p2/7406091.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1846116362?book_show_action=true">Dec 23, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Missy J" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Missy J" href="/user/show/7406091-missy-j">Missy J</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780435913533" href="/book/show/6335729-season-of-migration-to-the-north">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7406091-missy-j?shelf=africa-related">africa-related</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7406091-missy-j?shelf=2016-books">2016-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7406091-missy-j?shelf=greater-middle-east">greater-middle-east</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1846116362" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15192391071543823134"><blockquote>
  <i>By the standards of the European industrial world we are poor peasants, but when I embrace grandfather I experience a sense of richness as though I am a note in the heartbeats of the very universe.</i>
</blockquote><br/>Excellent writing! My reading of books translated from Arabic to English is severely limited. This year I read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/74305.Sinan_Antoon' title='Sinan Antoon' rel='nofollow noopener'>Sinan Antoon</a>'s <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16073290.The_Corpse_Washer' title='The Corpse Washer by Sinan Antoon' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Corpse Washer</a> and found it lyrical in a phenomenal way. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/70692.Tayeb_Salih' title='Tayeb Salih' rel='nofollow noopener'>Tayeb Salih</a>'s <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1398809.Season_of_Migration_to_the_North' title='Season of Migration to the North by Tayeb Salih' rel='nofollow noopener'>Season of Migration to the North</a> is equally very lyrical and evocative. Even though it is a short b</span>
  <span id="freeText15192391071543823134" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>By the standards of the European industrial world we are poor peasants, but when I embrace grandfather I experience a sense of richness as though I am a note in the heartbeats of the very universe.</i>
</blockquote><br />Excellent writing! My reading of books translated from Arabic to English is severely limited. This year I read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/74305.Sinan_Antoon" title="Sinan Antoon" rel="nofollow noopener">Sinan Antoon</a>'s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16073290.The_Corpse_Washer" title="The Corpse Washer by Sinan Antoon" rel="nofollow noopener">The Corpse Washer</a> and found it lyrical in a phenomenal way. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/70692.Tayeb_Salih" title="Tayeb Salih" rel="nofollow noopener">Tayeb Salih</a>'s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1398809.Season_of_Migration_to_the_North" title="Season of Migration to the North by Tayeb Salih" rel="nofollow noopener">Season of Migration to the North</a> is equally very lyrical and evocative. Even though it is a short book, it leaves the reader pondering. I feel inadequate to write a proper review for this book. Definitely plan to read this in the future again.<br /><br />The narrator in this novel is nameless. He returns to his village along the Nile river in the northern part of the newly independent Sudan after studying in England for seven years. Not much has changed in his village, except that a new, mysterious stranger named Mustafa Sa'eed has joined the village during his absence. Mustafa farms and lives with his wife and two sons, when on a drunken night the narrator catches him reciting a poem in English!<br /><br />Mustafa's story is extreme. I don't want to spoil the story but despite his brilliant mind in securing a scholarship to study in England, Mustafa engages in a lot stormy love affairs with British women that all end in tragedy and horror. This postcolonial work of fiction examines not only the power structure between the colonizer and the colonized. It also includes gender relations, be it in liberal England or in patriarchal Sudan. <br /><br />Call it a lie, a mirage or a phantom. Or maybe even better, <b>fata morgana</b>. Mustafa seduces the British women with Oriental fantasies. <i>"I was the invader who had come from the South, and this was the icy battlefield from which I would not make a safe return. [...] For me this moment of ecstasy is worth the whole life."</i> <br /><br />Throughout the novel, the author makes smart use of adjectives and nouns referring to hot and cold. Next time, I need to pay more attention to that. The depiction of Islam in this story is also noteworthy. At that time, the villagers didn't mind mixing Islam with their local customs (we find them drinking not a small amount of alcohol throughout the novel). In the end, I couldn't help but draw parallels between Mustafa's first marriage and what happened to Hosna. Just like the Nile River flows northwards, the influence that colonialism had on both the colonizer and the colonized becomes ubiquitous and its subsequent collision inevitable.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15192391071543823134" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1894583448" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1894583448" />
    <a title="Mira Jundi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41831542-mira-jundi"><img alt="Mira Jundi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1487015197p2/41831542.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1894583448?book_show_action=true">Feb 01, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mira Jundi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mira Jundi" href="/user/show/41831542-mira-jundi">Mira Jundi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1894583448" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14752006672149672776">What an astonishing novel!<br/><br/>In a different - but somehow similar- way, Salih and Conrad went deep down to represent colonialism in a breathtaking manner. In his Heart of Darkness, Conrad tells the story of a white man who goes through the Congo river to reach Africa, while in this novel, Salih tells the story of a black man who goes through the Thames river to reach England. However, the seeking reader is not able to find neither light nor darkness in both places.<br/><br/>Mustafa Saeed and the narrator -wh</span>
  <span id="freeText14752006672149672776" style="display:none">What an astonishing novel!<br /><br />In a different - but somehow similar- way, Salih and Conrad went deep down to represent colonialism in a breathtaking manner. In his Heart of Darkness, Conrad tells the story of a white man who goes through the Congo river to reach Africa, while in this novel, Salih tells the story of a black man who goes through the Thames river to reach England. However, the seeking reader is not able to find neither light nor darkness in both places.<br /><br />Mustafa Saeed and the narrator -whose name is not told- are two faces of one coin, unified in one figure with the author himself, in a way that shows the greatness of Salih and his smooth style.<br /><br />"There is a still pool in the depths of every woman that I knew how to stir."<br />I'm fascinated by the extraordinary way that Salih managed to represent the conflict between the colonized and the colonizer, drawing us a picture of the godlike figure of the black Mustafa, who could invade the body of any English woman he wants, creating this amazing analogy from his sexual encounters, ending with a passionate terminating sex with the one woman he could not seduce.<br /><br />I've enjoyed every part, sentence, and word, and I'm willing to read it again in its original language.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14752006672149672776" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2479479265" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2479479265" />
    <a title="Dax" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/72907701-dax"><img alt="Dax" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1573155046p2/72907701.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2479479265?book_show_action=true">Aug 04, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dax" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dax" href="/user/show/72907701-dax">Dax</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781590173022" href="/book/show/6145869-season-of-migration-to-the-north">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/72907701-dax?shelf=3-stars">3-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/72907701-dax?shelf=nyrb-classics">nyrb-classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/72907701-dax?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/72907701-dax?shelf=translated">translated</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2479479265" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14018497745965225241">A depressing book, but there are a lot of elegant passages and the author takes a hard look at colonialism and gender relations in post-colonial Sudan. I can understand why this is considered one of the most important novels to come out of Sudan in the 20th century. A good three stars</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_58069477" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/58069477" />
    <a title="Jenny (Reading Envy)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/68030-jenny-reading-envy"><img alt="Jenny (Reading Envy)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1455391853p2/68030.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/58069477?book_show_action=true">Jun 01, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jenny (Reading Envy)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jenny (Reading Envy)" href="/user/show/68030-jenny-reading-envy">Jenny (Reading Envy)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=translated">translated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=location-sudan">location-sudan</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=africa2016">africa2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=read2016">read2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=location-africa">location-africa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=around-the-world">around-the-world</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer58069477" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer605819758886242209">This book was one of the group reads for the Goodreads group "The World's Literature." It is also a book for me to check off Sudan from my dwindling list of African countries I still need to read a book from. <br/><br/>Originally published in Arabic in 1966 and translated into English in 1969, the narrator of this novel has recently returned to the village of his upbringing, after being educated in Cairo and London. His re-entry into his old life isn't completely seamless, and there is one man living ther</span>
  <span id="freeText605819758886242209" style="display:none">This book was one of the group reads for the Goodreads group "The World's Literature." It is also a book for me to check off Sudan from my dwindling list of African countries I still need to read a book from. <br /><br />Originally published in Arabic in 1966 and translated into English in 1969, the narrator of this novel has recently returned to the village of his upbringing, after being educated in Cairo and London. His re-entry into his old life isn't completely seamless, and there is one man living there who he doesn't know. Most of the novel is really more of the other man's stories, and they illuminate a lot of cultural differences without being preachy about anything. This is a different portrayal of village life than I've read recently, because the religious practice in this novel (Islam) is more just a facet of life. Female genital mutilation is mentioned but only from the male perspective (in a discussion of which country's women are better in bed! - another element I don't think you'd see from a 21st century Arabic country due to censorship). There are some comments on economy (and the leaders profiting instead of building schools), who should be educated, and family systems. <br /><br />The narrator's family still associates itself with a "tribe," which sets them apart from other families in the village. At one point they go to a Bedouin party, with their relations.<br /><br />A bit confusing at times, with a lot of intertwining stories that move fluidly in the timeline, but I liked seeing an earlier, different side of Sudan than I'd seen from either the colonial or 21st century perspective.</span>
  <a data-text-id="605819758886242209" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('1uf8ZThZJEV1xW0XZRunVcEd9pF4otiO3juSDDEPZm4T2Dc6TZEE0yi2YlmWfmL/1FWPErls91Lw1i4TAiK92w==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('KqnDKMmsZfap9d6X1+KvVa7M4YBKjCEuxO4sy6IIJHzvlgh3vGRFYPSG0dkkh2r/u4SYA4tCDvLqA5DUkSX/yQ==')})
  }
//]]>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer13772072147046759588">The Sudanese writer al-Tayyib Salih (Arabic: الطيب صالح)has been described as the "genius of the modern Arabic novel." He has lived abroad for most of his life, yet his fiction is firmly rooted in the village in which he spent his early years. His most well-known work is the modern classic Mawsim al-hijra ila’l-shamal (1967; Season of Migration to the North), which received great critical attentio</span>
  <span id="freeText13772072147046759588" style="display:none">The Sudanese writer al-Tayyib Salih (Arabic: الطيب صالح)has been described as the "genius of the modern Arabic novel." He has lived abroad for most of his life, yet his fiction is firmly rooted in the village in which he spent his early years. His most well-known work is the modern classic Mawsim al-hijra ila’l-shamal (1967; Season of Migration to the North), which received great critical attention and brought new vitality to the Arab novel.<br /><br />Salih has not been a prolific writer; his early work, including Season of Migration to the North, remains the best of his oeuvre. He has received critical acclaim in both the west and the east. In Sudan he is without rival, and his writing has played a considerable part in drawing attention to Sudanese literature. Arabic literature has been dominated by social criticism, social realism, and committed literature depicting the bitter realities of life; Salih managed to break with this trend and return to the roots of his culture, capturing the mystery, magic, humor, sorrows, and celebrations of rural life and popular religion.<br /><br />الطيب صالح أديب عربي من السودان ولد عام (1348هـ - 1929م ) في إقليم مروي شمالي السودان بقرية كَرْمَكوْل بالقرب من قرية دبة الفقراء وهي إحدى قرى قبيلة الركابية التي ينتسب إليها. عاش مطلع حياته وطفولته في ذلك الإقليم, وفي شبابه انتقل إلى الخرطوم لإكمال دراسته فحصل من جامعتها على درجة البكالوريوس في العلوم. سافر إلى إنجلترا حيث واصل دراسته, وغيّر تخصصه إلى دراسة الشؤون الدولية.<br />تنقل الطيب صالح بين عدة مواقع مهنية فعدا عن خبرة قصيرة في إدارة مدرسة، عمل الطيب صالح لسنوات طويلة من حياته في القسم العربي لهيئة الإذاعة البريطانية, وترقى بها حتى وصل إلى منصب مدير قسم الدراما, وبعد استقالته من البي بي سي عاد إلى السودان وعمل لفترة في الإذاعة السودانية, ثم هاجر إلى دولة قطر وعمل في وزارة إعلامها وكيلاً ومشرفاً على أجهزتها. عمل بعد ذلك مديراً إقليمياً بمنظمة اليونيسكو في باريس, وعمل ممثلاً لهذه المنظمة في الخليج العربي. ويمكن القول أن حالة الترحال والتنقل بين الشرق والغرب والشمال والجنوب أكسبته خبرة واسعة بأحوال الحياة والعالم وأهم من ذلك أحوال أمته وقضاياها وهو ما وظفه في كتاباته وأعماله الروائية وخاصة روايته العالمية "موسم الهجرة إلى الشمال".<br />كتب العديد من الروايات التي ترجمت إلى أكثر من ثلاثين لغة وهي « موسم الهجرة إلى الشمال» و«عرس الزين» و«مريود» و«ضو البيت» و«دومة ود حامد» و«منسى».. تعتبر روايته "موسم الهجرة إلى الشمال" واحدة من أفضل مائة رواية في العالم .. وقد حصلت على العديد من الجوائز .. وقد نشرت لأول مرة في اواخر الستينات من القرن ال-20 في بيروت وتم تتويجه ك"عبقري الادب العربي". في عام 2001 تم الاعتراف بكتابه على يد الاكاديميا العربية في دمشق على انه "الرواية العربية الأفضل في القرن ال-20.) أصدر الطيب صالح ثلاث روايات وعدة مجموعات قصصية قصير</span>
  <a data-text-id="13772072147046759588" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
